<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Brown dwarf</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:279px;max-width:279px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:277px;max-width:277px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="4268" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg/275px-2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg/413px-2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg/550px-2MASSJ22282889-431026.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Artist's concept of a T-type brown dwarf</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:277px;max-width:277px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="720" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg/275px-BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg/413px-BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg/550px-BrownDwarfComparison-pia12462.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Comparison: most brown dwarfs are only slightly larger than Jupiter (10–15%) but up to 80 times more massive due to greater density. The Sun is not to scale and would be larger.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Brown dwarfs</b> are <a href="/wiki/Substellar_object" title="Substellar object">substellar objects</a> that occupy the mass range between the heaviest <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> and the lightest <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, of approximately 13 to 75–80 <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass">Jupiter masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass"><i>M</i><sub>J</sub></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wethington_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wethington-2">[2]</a></sup> Below this range are the <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">sub-brown dwarfs</a>, and above it are the lightest <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a> (M9 V). Brown dwarfs may be fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective" title="Convective">convective</a>, with no layers or chemical differentiation by depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike the stars in the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a>, brown dwarfs are not massive enough to sustain <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> of ordinary <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (<a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen"><sup>1</sup>H</a>) to helium in their cores. They are, however, thought to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterium_burning" title="Deuterium burning">fuse deuterium</a> (<sup>2</sup>H) and to <a href="/wiki/Lithium_burning" title="Lithium burning">burn lithium</a> (<sup>7</sup>Li) if their mass is above a debated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT-June2008_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-PT-June2008-4">[4]</a></sup> threshold of <span class="nowrap">13</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub> and <span class="nowrap">65</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wethington_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wethington-2">[2]</a></sup> It is also debated whether brown dwarfs would be better defined by their formation processes rather than by their supposed nuclear fusion reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT-June2008_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-PT-June2008-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stars are categorized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_classification" title="Spectral classification">spectral class</a>, with brown dwarfs designated as types M, L, T, and Y.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT-June2008_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-PT-June2008-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MichaelCushing2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MichaelCushing2014-5">[5]</a></sup> Despite their name, brown dwarfs are of different colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT-June2008_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-PT-June2008-4">[4]</a></sup> Many brown dwarfs would likely appear <a href="/wiki/Magenta" title="Magenta">magenta</a> to the human eye,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PT-June2008_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-PT-June2008-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burrows2001_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows2001-6">[6]</a></sup> or possibly orange/red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cain-7">[7]</a></sup> Brown dwarfs are not very luminous at visible wavelengths.</p>
<p>Planets are known to orbit some brown dwarfs: <a href="/wiki/2M1207b" title="2M1207b">2M1207b</a>, <a href="/wiki/MOA-2007-BLG-192Lb" title="MOA-2007-BLG-192Lb">MOA-2007-BLG-192Lb</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2MASS_J044144b" title="2MASS J044144b">2MASS J044144b</a></p>
<p>At a distance of about 6.5 light years, the nearest known brown dwarf is <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">Luhman 16</a>, a binary system of brown dwarfs discovered in 2013. <a href="/wiki/DENIS-P_J082303.1-491201_b" title="DENIS-P J082303.1-491201 b">DENIS-P J082303.1-491201 b</a> is listed as <a href="/wiki/List_of_exoplanet_extremes#Planetary_characteristics" title="List of exoplanet extremes">the most-massive known exoplanet</a> (as of March 2014) in NASA's exoplanet archive, despite having a mass (<span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001285000000000000♠</span>28.5<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1.9</span></span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>) more than twice the 13-Jupiter-mass cutoff between planets and brown dwarfs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="800" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg/220px-Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg/330px-Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg/440px-Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Dwarf_Gliese_229B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The smaller object is Gliese 229B, about 20 to 50 times the mass of Jupiter, orbiting the star <a href="/wiki/Gliese_229" title="Gliese 229">Gliese 229</a>. It is in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Lepus_(constellation)" title="Lepus (constellation)">Lepus</a>, about 19 light years from Earth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The objects now called "brown dwarfs" were theorized to exist in the 1960s by Shiv S. Kumar and were originally called <a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarfs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> a classification for dark substellar objects floating freely in space that were not massive enough to sustain hydrogen fusion. However: a) the term <a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarf</a> was already in use to refer to a cold <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>; b) <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a> fuse hydrogen, and c) these objects may be luminous at visible wavelengths early in their lives. Because of this, alternate names for these objects were proposed, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetar_(astronomy)" title="Planetar (astronomy)">planetar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Substellar_object" title="Substellar object">substar</a>. In 1975, <a href="/wiki/Jill_Tarter" title="Jill Tarter">Jill Tarter</a> suggested the term "brown dwarf", using brown as an approximate color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cain-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JillTarter2014_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-JillTarter2014-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term black dwarf still refers to a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> that has cooled to the point that it no longer emits significant light. However, the time required for even the lowest-mass white dwarf <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf#Radiation_and_cooling" title="White dwarf">to cool to this temperature</a> is calculated to be longer than the current age of the universe; hence such objects are thought not to yet exist.</p>
<p>Early theories concerning the nature of the lowest-mass stars and the hydrogen-burning limit suggested that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_I" title="Population I">population I</a> object with a mass less than 0.07 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a>) or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_II" title="Population II">population II</a> object less than 0.09 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> would never go through normal <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">stellar evolution</a> and would become a completely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degenerate_star" title="Degenerate star">degenerate star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumar1963_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar1963-12">[12]</a></sup> The first self-consistent calculation of the hydrogen-burning minimum mass confirmed a value between 0.08 and 0.07 solar masses for population I objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayashi1963_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayashi1963-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nakano2014_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nakano2014-14">[14]</a></sup> The discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterium_burning" title="Deuterium burning">deuterium burning</a> down to 0.012 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> and the impact of dust formation in the cool outer <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of brown dwarfs in the late 1980s brought these theories into question. However, such objects were hard to find as they emit almost no visible light. Their strongest emissions are in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> (IR) spectrum, and ground-based IR detectors were too imprecise at that time to readily identify any brown dwarfs.</p>
<p>Since then, numerous searches by various methods have sought these objects. These methods included multi-color imaging surveys around field stars, imaging surveys for faint companions of <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> dwarfs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_dwarfs" title="White dwarfs">white dwarfs</a>, surveys of young star clusters, and <a href="/wiki/Radial_velocity" title="Radial velocity">radial velocity</a> monitoring for close companions.</p>
<p>For many years, efforts to discover brown dwarfs were fruitless. In 1988, however, a faint companion to a star known as <a href="/wiki/GD_165" title="GD 165">GD 165</a> was found in an infrared search of white dwarfs. The spectrum of the companion GD 165B was very red and enigmatic, showing none of the features expected of a low-mass <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a>. It became clear that GD 165B would need to be classified as a much cooler object than the latest M dwarfs then known. GD 165B remained unique for almost a decade until the advent of the Two Micron All Sky Survey (<a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a>) which discovered many objects with similar colors and spectral features.</p>
<p>Today, GD 165B is recognized as the prototype of a class of objects now called "L dwarfs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin1997_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1997-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkpatrick1999_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkpatrick1999-16">[16]</a></sup> Although the discovery of the coolest dwarf was highly significant at the time, it was debated whether GD 165B would be classified as a brown dwarf or simply a very-low-mass star, because observationally it is very difficult to distinguish between the two.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Soon after the discovery of GD 165B, other brown-dwarf candidates were reported. Most failed to live up to their candidacy, however, because the absence of lithium showed them to be stellar objects. True stars <a href="/wiki/Lithium_burning" title="Lithium burning">burn their lithium</a> within a little over 100 <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">Myr</a>, whereas brown dwarfs (which can, confusingly, have temperatures and luminosities similar to true stars) will not. Hence, the detection of lithium in the atmosphere of an object older than 100 Myr ensures that it is a brown dwarf.</p>
<p>In 1995, the study of brown dwarfs changed substantially with the discovery of two indisputable substellar objects (<a href="/wiki/Teide_1" title="Teide 1">Teide 1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_229B" title="Gliese 229B">Gliese 229B</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RafaelRebolo2014_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-RafaelRebolo2014-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BenOppenheimer_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BenOppenheimer-18">[18]</a></sup> which were identified by the presence of the 670.8 nm lithium line. The latter was found to have a temperature and luminosity well below the stellar range. Its near-infrared spectrum clearly exhibited a methane absorption band at 2 micrometres, a feature that had previously only been observed in the atmospheres of giant planets and that of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. Methane absorption is not expected at the temperatures of main-sequence stars. This discovery helped to establish yet another spectral class even cooler than L dwarfs, known as "T dwarfs", for which Gliese 229B is the prototype.</p>
<p>The first confirmed brown dwarf was discovered by Spanish astrophysicists <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rafael_Rebolo_L%C3%B3pez" title="es:Rafael Rebolo López">Rafael Rebolo</a> (head of team), Maria Rosa Zapatero Osorio, and Eduardo Martín in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This object, found in the <a href="/wiki/Pleiades" title="Pleiades">Pleiades</a> open cluster, received the name <a href="/wiki/Teide_1" title="Teide 1">Teide 1</a>. The discovery article was submitted to <i>Nature</i> in spring 1995, and published on September 14, 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RafaelRebolo2014_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-RafaelRebolo2014-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <i>Nature</i> highlighted "Brown dwarfs discovered, official" in the front page of that issue.</p>
<p>Teide 1 was discovered in images collected by the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Canarias" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Canarias">IAC</a> team on January 6, 1994 using the 80 cm telescope (IAC 80) at <a href="/wiki/Teide_Observatory" title="Teide Observatory">Teide Observatory</a> and its spectrum was first recorded in December 1994 using the 4.2 m William Herschel Telescope at <a href="/wiki/Roque_de_los_Muchachos_Observatory" title="Roque de los Muchachos Observatory">Roque de los Muchachos Observatory</a> (La Palma). The distance, chemical composition, and age of Teide 1 could be established because of its membership in the young Pleiades star cluster. Using the most advanced stellar and substellar evolution models at that moment, the team estimated for Teide 1 a mass of <span class="nowrap">55</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which is below the stellar-mass limit. The object became a reference in subsequent young brown dwarf related works.</p>
<p>In theory, a brown dwarf below <span class="nowrap">65</span> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass"><i>M</i><sub>J</sub></a> is unable to burn lithium by thermonuclear fusion at any time during its evolution. This fact is one of the lithium test principles used to judge the substellar nature of low-luminosity and low-surface-temperature astronomical bodies.</p>
<p>High-quality spectral data acquired by the Keck 1 telescope in November 1995 showed that Teide 1 still had the initial lithium abundance of the original molecular cloud from which Pleiades stars formed, proving the lack of thermonuclear fusion in its core. These observations confirmed that Teide 1 is a brown dwarf, as well as the efficiency of the spectroscopic <a href="/wiki/Lithium_burning" title="Lithium burning">lithium test</a>.</p>
<p>For some time, Teide 1 was the smallest known object outside the Solar System that had been identified by direct observation. Since then, over 1,800 brown dwarfs have been identified,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DwarfArchives_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-DwarfArchives-21">[21]</a></sup> even some very close to Earth like <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Indi" title="Epsilon Indi">Epsilon Indi</a> Ba and Bb, a pair of brown dwarfs gravitationally bound to a Sun-like star 12 light-years from the Sun, and <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">Luhman 16</a>, a binary system of brown dwarfs at 6.5 light-years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="img" style="width: 400px; float: right; clear: right; line-height: 1; text-align: center; background-color: #ffffff; position: relative;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HR-diag-no-text-2.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="512" height="457" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/400px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/600px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/800px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div style="position:absolute; left:74px; top:392px"><a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram"><span style="color: #000080; font-size: 110%">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:182px; top:432px"><a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification"><span style="color: #000080; font-size: 120%">Spectral type</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:322px; top:364px"><strong class="selflink"><span style="color: white; font-size: 100%">Brown dwarfs</span></strong></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:154px; top:316px"><a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">White dwarfs</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:334px; top:300px"><a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf"><span style="color: white; font-size: 100%">Red dwarfs</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:202px; top:268px"><a href="/wiki/Subdwarf" title="Subdwarf"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Subdwarfs</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:94px; top:222px"><a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Main sequence<br/>
("dwarfs")</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:262px; top:184px"><a href="/wiki/Subgiant" title="Subgiant"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Subgiants</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:174px; top:164px"><a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Giants</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:194px; top:132px"><a href="/wiki/Bright_giant" title="Bright giant"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Bright giants</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:174px; top:104px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Supergiants</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:174px; top:71.2px"><a href="/wiki/Hypergiant" title="Hypergiant"><span style="color: black; font-size: 100%">Hypergiants</span></a></div>
<div style="position:absolute; left:2px; top:212px"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude"><span style="color: #000080; font-size: 100%">absolute<br/>
magni-<br/>
tude<br/>
(M<sub>V</sub>)</span></a></div>
</div>
<p>The standard mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">star birth</a> is through the gravitational collapse of a cold interstellar cloud of gas and dust. As the cloud contracts it heats due to the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism">Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism</a>. Early in the process the contracting gas quickly radiates away much of the energy, allowing the collapse to continue. Eventually, the central region becomes sufficiently dense to trap radiation. Consequently, the central temperature and density of the collapsed cloud increases dramatically with time, slowing the contraction, until the conditions are hot and dense enough for thermonuclear reactions to occur in the core of the <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostar</a>. For most stars, gas and radiation pressure generated by the <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a> reactions within the core of the star will support it against any further gravitational contraction. <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">Hydrostatic equilibrium</a> is reached and the star will spend most of its lifetime fusing hydrogen into helium as a main-sequence star.</p>
<p>If, however, the mass of the protostar is less than about 0.08 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>, normal hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a> reactions will not ignite in the core. Gravitational contraction does not heat the small <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostar</a> very effectively, and before the temperature in the core can increase enough to trigger fusion, the density reaches the point where electrons become closely packed enough to create quantum <a href="/wiki/Electron_degeneracy_pressure" title="Electron degeneracy pressure">electron degeneracy pressure</a>. According to the brown dwarf interior models, typical conditions in the core for density, temperature and pressure are expected to be the following:</p>

</ul>
<p>This means that the protostar is not massive enough and not dense enough to ever reach the conditions needed to sustain hydrogen fusion. The infalling matter is prevented, by electron degeneracy pressure, from reaching the densities and pressures needed.</p>
<p>Further gravitational contraction is prevented and the result is a "failed star", or brown dwarf that simply cools off by radiating away its internal thermal energy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-mass_brown_dwarfs_vs._low-mass_stars">High-mass brown dwarfs vs. low-mass stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: High-mass brown dwarfs vs. low-mass stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Lithium</a> is generally present in brown dwarfs and not in low-mass stars. Stars, which reach the high temperature necessary for fusing hydrogen, rapidly deplete their lithium. Fusion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-7" title="Lithium-7">lithium-7</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> occurs producing two <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a> nuclei. The temperature necessary for this reaction is just below that necessary for hydrogen fusion. Convection in low-mass stars ensures that lithium in the whole volume of the star is eventually depleted. Therefore, the presence of the lithium spectral line in a candidate brown dwarf is a strong indicator that it is indeed a substellar object. The use of lithium to distinguish candidate brown dwarfs from low-mass stars is commonly referred to as the <b>lithium test</b>, and was pioneered by <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rafael_Rebolo_L%C3%B3pez" title="es:Rafael Rebolo López">Rafael Rebolo</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eduardo_Mart%C3%ADn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eduardo Martín (page does not exist)">Eduardo Martín</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antonio_Magazzu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antonio Magazzu (page does not exist)">Antonio Magazzu</a>. However, lithium is also seen in very young stars, which have not yet had enough time to burn it all. Heavier stars, like the Sun, can retain lithium in their outer atmospheres, which never get hot enough and the convective layer does not mix with the core where the lithium would be rapidly depleted. Those larger stars are also distinguishable from brown dwarfs by their size and luminosity. On the contrary, brown dwarfs at the high end of their mass range can be hot enough to deplete their lithium when they are young. Dwarfs of mass greater than <span class="nowrap">65</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub> can burn their lithium by the time they are half a billion years old,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> thus the lithium test is not perfect.</p>
<p>Unlike stars, older brown dwarfs are sometimes cool enough that, over very long periods of time, their atmospheres can gather observable quantities of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> which cannot form in hotter objects. Dwarfs confirmed in this fashion include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_229B" title="Gliese 229B">Gliese 229B</a>.</p>
<p>Main-sequence stars cool, but eventually reach a minimum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolometric_luminosity" title="Bolometric luminosity">bolometric luminosity</a> that they can sustain through steady fusion. This varies from star to star, but is generally at least 0.01% that of the Sun.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="as in talk page (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Brown dwarfs cool and darken steadily over their lifetimes: sufficiently old brown dwarfs will be too faint to be detectable.</p>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Iron_rain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Iron rain (page does not exist)">Iron rain</a> as part of atmospheric convection processes is possible only in brown dwarfs, and not in small stars. The spectroscopy research into iron rain is still ongoing, but not all brown dwarfs will always display this atmospheric anomaly. In 2013, a heterogeneous iron-containing atmosphere was imaged around the B component in the close <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">Luhman 16</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-mass_brown_dwarfs_vs._high-mass_planets">Low-mass brown dwarfs vs. high-mass planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Low-mass brown dwarfs vs. high-mass planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="701" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png/220px-Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png/330px-Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png/440px-Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brown_Dwarf_HD_29587_B.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An artistic concept of the brown dwarf around the star <a href="/wiki/HD_29587" title="HD 29587">HD 29587</a>, a companion known as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=HD_29587_B&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="HD 29587 B (page does not exist)">HD 29587 B</a>, and estimated to be about 55 Jupiter masses.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brown dwarfs are all roughly the same radius as Jupiter. At the high end of their mass range (<span class="nowrap">60–90</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>), the volume of a brown dwarf is governed primarily by <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">electron-degeneracy</a> pressure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> as it is in white dwarfs; at the low end of the range (<span class="nowrap">10</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>), their volume is governed primarily by <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">Coulomb pressure</a>, as it is in planets. The net result is that the radii of brown dwarfs vary by only 10–15% over the range of possible masses. This can make distinguishing them from planets difficult.</p>
<p>In addition, many brown dwarfs undergo no fusion; those at the low end of the mass range (under <span class="nowrap">13</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>) are never hot enough to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterium_burning" title="Deuterium burning">fuse even deuterium</a>, and even those at the high end of the mass range (over <span class="nowrap">60</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>) cool quickly enough that after 10 million years they no longer undergo fusion.</p>
<p>X-ray and infrared spectra are telltale signs of brown dwarfs. Some emit <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>; and all "warm" dwarfs continue to glow tellingly in the red and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> spectra until they cool to planet-like temperatures (under 1000 K).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">Gas giants</a> have some of the characteristics of brown dwarfs. Like the Sun, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> are both made primarily of hydrogen and helium. Saturn is nearly as large as Jupiter, despite having only 30% the mass. Three of the giant planets in the Solar System (Jupiter, Saturn, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>) emit much more heat than they receive from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> And all four giant planets have their own "planetary systems"—their moons. Like stars, brown dwarfs form independently, but lack sufficient mass to "ignite" as do stars. Like all stars, they can occur singly or in close proximity to other stars. Some orbit stars and can, like planets, have eccentric orbits.</p>
<p>Currently, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> considers an object above <span class="nowrap">13</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>, which is the limiting mass for thermonuclear fusion of deuterium, to be a brown dwarf, whereas an object under that mass (and orbiting a star or stellar remnant) is considered a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 13 Jupiter-mass cutoff is a rule of thumb rather than something of precise physical significance. Larger objects will burn most of their deuterium and smaller ones will burn only a little, and the 13 Jupiter mass value is somewhere in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bodenheimer2013_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-bodenheimer2013-27">[27]</a></sup> The amount of deuterium burnt also depends to some extent on the composition of the object, specifically on the amount of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> present and on the fraction of heavier elements, which determines the atmospheric opacity and thus the radiative cooling rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spiegel2011_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spiegel2011-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Extrasolar_Planets_Encyclopaedia" title="Extrasolar Planets Encyclopaedia">Extrasolar Planets Encyclopaedia</a> includes objects up to 25 Jupiter masses, and the <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet_Data_Explorer" title="Exoplanet Data Explorer">Exoplanet Data Explorer</a> up to 24 Jupiter masses.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-brown_dwarf"><span id="Sub-brown_dwarf"></span> Sub-brown dwarf</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sub-brown dwarf">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">Sub-brown dwarf</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg/220px-Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg/330px-Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg/440px-Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sol_Cha-110913-773444_Jupiter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A size comparison between the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, a young sub-brown dwarf, and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. As the sub-brown dwarf ages, it will gradually cool and shrink</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Objects below <span class="nowrap">13</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>, called <b>sub-brown dwarf</b> or <b>planetary-mass brown dwarf</b>, form in the same manner as <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and brown dwarfs (i.e. through the collapse of a <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">gas cloud</a>) but have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary-mass_object" title="Planetary-mass object">mass below the limiting mass for thermonuclear fusion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Some researchers call them free-floating planets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delorme2012_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delorme2012-30">[30]</a></sup> whereas others call them planetary-mass brown dwarfs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luhman20140421_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luhman20140421-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations">Observations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Observations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_brown_dwarfs">Classification of brown dwarfs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Classification of brown dwarfs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_class_M">Spectral class M</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Spectral class M">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/220px-Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/330px-Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/440px-Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Late-M-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's vision of a late-M dwarf</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are brown dwarfs with a spectral class of M6.5 or later. They are also called late-M dwarfs.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_class_L">Spectral class L</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Spectral class L">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="366" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/220px-L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/330px-L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's vision of an L-dwarf</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The defining characteristic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_class" title="Spectral class">spectral class</a> M, the coolest type in the long-standing classical stellar sequence, is an optical spectrum dominated by absorption bands of <a href="/wiki/Titanium(II)_oxide" title="Titanium(II) oxide">titanium(II) oxide</a> (TiO) and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium(II)_oxide" title="Vanadium(II) oxide">vanadium(II) oxide</a> (VO) molecules. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GD_165B" title="GD 165B">GD 165B</a>, the cool companion to the white dwarf <a href="/wiki/GD_165" title="GD 165">GD 165</a>, had none of the hallmark TiO features of M dwarfs. The subsequent identification of many objects like GD 165B ultimately led to the definition of a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_class" title="Spectral class">spectral class</a>, the <b>L dwarfs</b>, defined in the red optical region of the spectrum not by absorption metal-oxide bands (TiO, VO), but metal <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> emission bands (<a href="/wiki/Iron(I)_hydride" title="Iron(I) hydride">FeH</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium_hydride" title="Chromium hydride">CrH</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_hydride" title="Magnesium hydride">MgH</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydride" title="Calcium hydride">CaH</a>) and prominent <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> lines (Na I, K I, Cs I, Rb I). As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, over 900 L dwarfs have been identified,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DwarfArchives_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-DwarfArchives-21">[21]</a></sup> most by wide-field surveys: the Two Micron All Sky Survey (<a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a>), the Deep Near Infrared Survey of the Southern Sky (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Near_Infrared_Survey" title="Deep Near Infrared Survey">DENIS</a>), and the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (<a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">SDSS</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_class_T">Spectral class T</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Spectral class T">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/220px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/330px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/440px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's vision of a T-dwarf</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As GD 165B is the prototype of the L dwarfs, <a href="/wiki/Gliese_229" title="Gliese 229">Gliese 229</a>B is the prototype of a second new spectral class, the <b>T dwarfs</b>. Whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">near-infrared</a> (NIR) spectra of L dwarfs show strong absorption bands of H<sub>2</sub>O and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO), the NIR spectrum of Gliese 229B is dominated by absorption bands from <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), features that were only found in the giant planets of the Solar System and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. CH<sub>4</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, and molecular <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (H<sub>2</sub>) collision-induced absorption (CIA) give Gliese 229B blue near-infrared colors. Its steeply sloped red optical spectrum also lacks the FeH and CrH bands that characterize L dwarfs and instead is influenced by exceptionally broad absorption features from the <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a> metals <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">Na</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">K</a>. These differences led Kirkpatrick to propose the T spectral class for objects exhibiting H- and K-band CH<sub>4</sub> absorption. As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 355 T dwarfs are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DwarfArchives_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-DwarfArchives-21">[21]</a></sup> NIR classification schemes for T dwarfs have recently been developed by Adam Burgasser and Tom Geballe. Theory suggests that L dwarfs are a mixture of very-low-mass stars and sub-stellar objects (brown dwarfs), whereas the T dwarf class is composed entirely of brown dwarfs. Because of the absorption of <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> in the green part of the spectrum of T dwarfs, the actual appearance of T dwarfs to human <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visual perception</a> is estimated to be not brown, but the color of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magenta_(color)#Magenta_dye_.281860.29" title="Magenta (color)">magenta coal tar dye</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burrows_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-burrows-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> T-class brown dwarfs, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WISE_0316%2B4307" title="WISE 0316+4307">WISE 0316+4307</a>, have been detected over 100 light-years from the Sun.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_class_Y">Spectral class Y</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spectral class Y">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/220px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/330px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/440px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's vision of a Y-dwarf</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is some doubt as to what, if anything, should be included in the class <b>Y dwarfs</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_Burningham_2008_pp._320_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben_Burningham_2008_pp._320-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luhman2011_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luhman2011-35">[35]</a></sup> They are expected to be much cooler than T-dwarfs. They have been modelled,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> though there is no well-defined spectral sequence yet with prototypes.</p>
<p>In 2009, the coolest known brown dwarfs had estimated effective temperatures between 500 and 600 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>, and have been assigned the spectral class T9. Three examples are the brown dwarfs <a href="/wiki/CFBDS_J005910.90-011401.3" title="CFBDS J005910.90-011401.3">CFBDS J005910.90-011401.3</a>, <a href="/wiki/ULAS_J133553.45%2B113005.2" title="ULAS J133553.45+113005.2">ULAS J133553.45+113005.2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ULAS_J003402.77%E2%88%92005206.7" title="ULAS J003402.77−005206.7">ULAS J003402.77−005206.7</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-four600k-37">[37]</a></sup> The spectra of these objects display absorption around 1.55 <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">micrometers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-four600k-37">[37]</a></sup> Delorme et al. have suggested that this feature is due to absorption from <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and that this should be taken as indicating the T–Y transition, making these objects of type Y0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-four600k-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tytrans_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-tytrans-38">[38]</a></sup> However, the feature is difficult to distinguish from absorption by water and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-four600k-37">[37]</a></sup> and other authors have stated that the assignment of class Y0 is premature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_Burningham_2008_pp._320_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ben_Burningham_2008_pp._320-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2010, two newly discovered ultracool <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">sub-brown dwarfs</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UGPS_0722-05" title="UGPS 0722-05">UGPS 0722-05</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SDWFS_1433%2B35&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SDWFS 1433+35 (page does not exist)">SDWFS 1433+35</a>) were proposed as prototypes for spectral class Y0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2011, Luhman et al. reported the discovery of a "brown dwarf" companion to a nearby white dwarf with a temperature of <span class="nowrap">c. 300 K</span> and mass of <span class="nowrap">7</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luhman2011_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luhman2011-35">[35]</a></sup> Though of planetary mass, Rodriguez et al. suggest it is unlikely to have formed in the same manner as planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodriguez2011_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez2011-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after that, Liu et al. published an account of a "very cold" <span class="nowrap">(c. 370 K)</span> brown dwarf orbiting another very-low-mass brown dwarf and noted that "Given its low luminosity, atypical colors and cold temperature, CFBDS J1458+10B is a promising candidate for the hypothesized Y spectral class."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu_et_al._2011_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liu_et_al._2011-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2011, scientists using data from NASA's <a href="/wiki/Wide-field_Infrared_Survey_Explorer" title="Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer">Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</a> (WISE) discovered six "Y dwarfs"—star-like bodies with temperatures as cool as the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plait2011_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plait2011-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA2011_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA2011-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="534" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg/220px-WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg/330px-WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg/440px-WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WISE2010-040-rotate180.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WISEPC_J045853.90%2B643451.9" title="WISEPC J045853.90+643451.9">WISE 0458+6434</a> is the first ultra-cool brown dwarf (green dot) discovered by <a href="/wiki/Wide-field_Infrared_Survey_Explorer" title="Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer">WISE</a>. The green and blue comes from infrared wavelengths mapped to visible colors.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>WISE data has revealed hundreds of new brown dwarfs. Of these, fourteen are classified as cool Ys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DwarfArchives_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-DwarfArchives-21">[21]</a></sup> One of the Y dwarfs, called <a href="/wiki/WISE_1828%2B2650" title="WISE 1828+2650">WISE 1828+2650</a>, was, as of August 2011, the record holder for the coldest brown dwarf – emitting no visible light at all, this type of object resembles free-floating planets more than stars. WISE 1828+2650 was initially estimated to have an atmospheric temperature cooler than 300 K<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>—for comparison, the upper end of <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> is 298 K (25 °C; 77 °F). Its temperature has since been revised and newer estimates put it in the range of 250 to 400 K (−23 to 127 °C; −10 to 260 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beichman2013_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beichman2013-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2014, <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 0855−0714">WISE 0855−0714</a> was announced with a temperature profile estimated around 225 to 260 K and a mass of <span class="nowrap">3 to 10</span> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass"><i>M</i><sub>J</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA20140425_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA20140425-46">[46]</a></sup> It was also unusual in that its observed parallax meant a distance close to 7.2±0.7 light years from the Solar System.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_and_atmospheric_properties_of_brown_dwarfs">Spectral and atmospheric properties of brown dwarfs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Spectral and atmospheric properties of brown dwarfs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The majority of flux emitted by L and T dwarfs is in the 1 to 2.5 micrometre near-infrared range. Low and decreasing temperatures through the late M-, L-, and T-dwarf sequence result in a rich near-infrared <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectrum</a> containing a wide variety of features, from relatively narrow lines of neutral atomic species to broad molecular bands, all of which have different dependencies on temperature, gravity, and <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>. Furthermore, these low temperature conditions favor condensation out of the gas state and the formation of grains.</p>
<p>Typical atmospheres of known brown dwarfs range in temperature from 2200 down to 750 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burrows_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-burrows-32">[32]</a></sup> Compared to stars, which warm themselves with steady internal fusion, brown dwarfs cool quickly over time; more massive dwarfs cool slower than less massive ones.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_techniques">Observational techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Observational techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png/220px-BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png/330px-BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png/440px-BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BrownDwarfs_Comparison_01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brown dwarfs <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Teide_1" title="Teide 1">Teide 1</a></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_229B" title="Gliese 229B">Gliese 229B</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WISE_1828%2B2650" title="WISE 1828+2650">WISE 1828+2650</a> compared to red dwarf <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliese_229A" title="Gliese 229A">Gliese 229A</a>, Jupiter and our Sun</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coronagraph" title="Coronagraph">Coronagraphs</a> have recently been used to detect faint objects orbiting bright visible stars, including Gliese 229B.</p>
<p>Sensitive telescopes equipped with charge-coupled devices (CCDs) have been used to search distant star clusters for faint objects, including Teide 1.</p>
<p>Wide-field searches have identified individual faint objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Kelu-1" title="Kelu-1">Kelu-1</a> (30 ly away).</p>
<p>Brown dwarfs are often discovered in surveys to discover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methods_of_detecting_extrasolar_planets" title="Methods of detecting extrasolar planets">Methods of detecting extrasolar planets</a> work for brown dwarfs as well, although brown dwarfs are much easier to detect.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Milestones">Milestones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Milestones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>1995: First brown dwarf verified. <a href="/wiki/Teide_1" title="Teide 1">Teide 1</a>, an M8 object in the <a href="/wiki/Pleiades" title="Pleiades">Pleiades</a> <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">cluster</a>, is picked out with a CCD in the Spanish Observatory of Roque de los Muchachos of the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Canarias" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Canarias">Instituto de Astrofísica de Canarias</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>First methane brown dwarf verified. Gliese 229B is discovered orbiting red dwarf <a href="/wiki/Gliese_229" title="Gliese 229">Gliese 229</a>A (20 ly away) using an <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> coronagraph to sharpen images from the 60-inch (1.5 m) reflecting telescope at <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a> on Southern California's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mt._Palomar" title="Mt. Palomar">Mt. Palomar</a>; follow-up infrared spectroscopy made with their 200-inch (5 m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hale_telescope" title="Hale telescope">Hale telescope</a> shows an abundance of methane.</p>
<ul>
<li>1998: First X-ray-emitting brown dwarf found. Cha Halpha 1, an M8 object in the <a href="/wiki/Chamaeleon_complex" title="Chamaeleon complex">Chamaeleon I</a> dark cloud, is determined to be an X-ray source, similar to convective late-type stars.</li>
<li>15 December 1999: First X-ray flare detected from a brown dwarf. A team at the University of California monitoring LP 944-20 (<span class="nowrap">60</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>, 16 ly away) via the <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a>, catches a 2-hour flare.</li>
<li>27 July 2000: First radio emission (in flare and quiescence) detected from a brown dwarf. A team of students at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_Large_Array" title="Very Large Array">Very Large Array</a> reported their observations of LP 944-20 in the 15 March 2001 issue of the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>.</li>
<li>25 April 2014: Coldest known brown dwarf discovered. <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 0855−0714">WISE 0855−0714</a> is 7.2 light-years away (7th closest system to the Sun) and has a temperature between −48 to −13 degrees Celsius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brown_dwarf_as_an_X-ray_source">Brown dwarf as an X-ray source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Brown dwarf as an X-ray source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lp94420_duo_m.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="500" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lp94420_duo_m.jpg/300px-Lp94420_duo_m.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lp94420_duo_m.jpg/450px-Lp94420_duo_m.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Lp94420_duo_m.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lp94420_duo_m.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra</a> image of LP 944-20 before flare and during flare</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>X-ray flares detected from brown dwarfs since 1999 suggest changing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_celestial_bodies" title="Magnetic field of celestial bodies">magnetic fields</a> within them, similar to those in very-low-mass stars.</p>
<p>With no strong central nuclear energy source, the interior of a brown dwarf is in a rapid boiling, or convective state. When combined with the rapid rotation that most brown dwarfs exhibit, <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> sets up conditions for the development of a strong, tangled <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> near the surface. The flare observed by <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra</a> from LP 944-20 could have its origin in the turbulent magnetized hot material beneath the brown dwarf's surface. A sub-surface flare could conduct heat to the atmosphere, allowing electric currents to flow and produce an X-ray flare, like a stroke of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>. The absence of X-rays from LP 944-20 during the non-flaring period is also a significant result. It sets the lowest observational limit on steady X-ray power produced by a brown dwarf, and shows that coronas cease to exist as the surface temperature of a brown dwarf cools below about 2800K and becomes electrically neutral.</p>
<p>Using NASA's <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray Observatory</a>, scientists have detected X-rays from a low-mass brown dwarf in a multiple star system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-48">[48]</a></sup> This is the first time that a brown dwarf this close to its parent star(s) (Sun-like stars TWA 5A) has been resolved in X-rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-48">[48]</a></sup> "Our Chandra data show that the X-rays originate from the brown dwarf's coronal plasma which is some 3 million degrees Celsius", said Yohko Tsuboi of <a href="/wiki/Chuo_University" title="Chuo University">Chuo University</a> in Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-48">[48]</a></sup> "This brown dwarf is as bright as the Sun today in X-ray light, while it is fifty times less massive than the Sun", said Tsuboi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-48">[48]</a></sup> "This observation, thus, raises the possibility that even massive planets might emit X-rays by themselves during their youth!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_developments">Recent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Recent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The brown dwarf <a href="/wiki/Cha_110913-773444" title="Cha 110913-773444">Cha 110913-773444</a>, located 500 light years away in the constellation Chamaeleon, may be in the process of forming a miniature planetary system. Astronomers from <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a> have detected what they believe to be a disk of gas and dust similar to the one hypothesized to have formed the Solar System. Cha 110913-773444 is the smallest brown dwarf found to date (<span class="nowrap">8</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub>), and if it formed a planetary system, it would be the smallest known object to have one. Their findings were published in the December 10, 2005 issue of Astrophysical Journal Letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent observations of known brown dwarf candidates have revealed a pattern of brightening and dimming of infrared emissions that suggests relatively cool, opaque cloud patterns obscuring a hot interior that is stirred by extreme winds. The weather on such bodies is thought to be extremely violent, comparable to but far exceeding Jupiter's famous storms.</p>
<p>On January 8, 2013 astronomers using NASA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_space_telescope" title="Hubble space telescope">Hubble</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitzer_space_telescope" title="Spitzer space telescope">Spitzer</a> space telescopes probed the stormy atmosphere of a brown dwarf named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2MASS_J22282889-431026" title="2MASS J22282889-431026">2MASS J22282889-431026</a>, creating the most detailed "weather map" of a brown dwarf thus far. It shows wind-driven, planet-sized clouds. The new research is a stepping stone toward a better understanding not only brown dwarfs, but also of the atmospheres of planets beyond the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Wide-field_Infrared_Survey_Explorer" title="Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer">WISE</a> mission has detected 200 new brown dwarfs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space2012_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-space2012-51">[51]</a></sup> There are actually fewer brown dwarfs in our cosmic neighborhood than previously thought. Rather than one star for every brown dwarf, there may be as many as six stars for every brown dwarf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space2012_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-space2012-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planets_around_brown_dwarfs">Planets around brown dwarfs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Planets around brown dwarfs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2475" data-file-width="4000" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg/220px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg/330px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg/440px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_disc_of_dust_and_gas_around_a_brown_dwarf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of a disc of dust and gas around a brown dwarf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Super-Jupiter" title="Super-Jupiter">super-Jupiter</a> planetary-mass objects <a href="/wiki/2M1207b" title="2M1207b">2M1207b</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2MASS_J044144" title="2MASS J044144">2MASS J044144</a> that are orbiting brown dwarfs at large orbital distances may have formed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloud_collapse" title="Cloud collapse">cloud collapse</a> rather than accretion and so may be <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">sub-brown dwarfs</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, which is inferred from relatively large masses and large orbits. The first discovery of a low-mass companion orbiting a brown dwarf (<a href="/wiki/ChaH%CE%B18" title="ChaHα8">ChaHα8</a>) at a small orbital distance using the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_spectroscopy" title="Doppler spectroscopy">radial velocity technique</a> paved the way for the detection of planets around brown dwarfs on orbits of a few AU or smaller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> However, with a mass ratio between the companion and primary in <a href="/wiki/ChaH%CE%B18" title="ChaHα8">ChaHα8</a> of about 0.3, this system rather resembles a binary star. Then, in 2013, the first planetary-mass companion (OGLE-2012-BLG-0358L b) in a relatively small orbit was discovered orbiting a brown dwarf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In 2015, the first terrestrial-mass planet orbiting a brown dwarf was found, OGLE-2013-BLG-0723LBb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disk" title="Circumstellar disk">Disks</a> around brown dwarfs have been found to have many of the same features as disks around stars; therefore, it is expected that there will be accretion-formed planets around brown dwarfs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Given the small mass of brown dwarf disks, most planets will be terrestrial planets rather than gas giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tidalplanets_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-tidalplanets-58">[58]</a></sup> If a giant planet orbits a brown dwarf across our line of sight, then, because they have approximately the same diameter, this would give a large signal for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">detection by transit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The accretion zone for planets around a brown dwarf is very close to the brown dwarf itself, so tidal forces would have a strong effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tidalplanets_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-tidalplanets-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Planets around brown dwarfs are likely to be <a href="/wiki/Carbon_planet" title="Carbon planet">carbon planets</a> depleted of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability">Habitability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Habitability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Habitability for hypothetical planets orbiting brown dwarfs has been studied. Computer models suggesting conditions for these bodies to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_planet" title="Habitable planet">habitable planets</a> are very stringent, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> being narrow and decreasing with time, due to the cooling of the brown dwarf. The <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> there would have to be of <i>very</i> low <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a> (of the order of 10<sup>−6</sup>) to avoid strong <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> that would trigger a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> on the planets, rendering them uninhabitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Superlative_brown_dwarfs">Superlative brown dwarfs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Superlative brown dwarfs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_brown_dwarfs" title="List of brown dwarfs">List of brown dwarfs</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/WD_0137-349" title="WD 0137-349">WD 0137-349</a> B: first confirmed brown dwarf to have survived the primary's <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1984, it was postulated by some astronomers that the Sun may be orbited by an undetected brown dwarf (sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(hypothetical_star)" title="Nemesis (hypothetical star)">Nemesis</a>) that could interact with the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> just as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars#Future_and_past" title="List of nearest stars">passing stars</a> can. However, this theory has fallen out of favor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrison_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrison-63">[63]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>S. S. Kumar, <i>Low-Luminosity Stars</i>. Gordon and Breach, London, 1969—an early overview paper on brown dwarfs</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Details">Details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.dwarfarchives.org/" rel="nofollow">A current list of L and T dwarfs</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://astron.berkeley.edu/~stars/bdwarfs/structbd.html" rel="nofollow">A geological definition of brown dwarfs</a>, contrasted with stars and planets (via Berkeley)</li>
<li>Neill Reid's pages at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute" title="Space Telescope Science Institute">Space Telescope Science Institute</a>:
<ul>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www-int.stsci.edu/~inr/ldwarf1.html" rel="nofollow">On spectral analysis</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M_dwarf" title="M dwarf">M dwarfs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L_dwarf" title="L dwarf">L dwarfs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_dwarf" title="T dwarf">T dwarfs</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www-int.stsci.edu/~inr/ldwarf3.html" rel="nofollow">Temperature and mass characteristics</a> of low-temperature dwarfs</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.spaceref.com/news/viewpr.html?pid=2192" rel="nofollow">First X-ray from brown dwarf observed</a>, Spaceref.com, 2000</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ucm.es/info/Astrof/recopilaciones/enanasmarrones.html" rel="nofollow">Brown Dwarfs and ultracool dwarfs (late-M, L, T)</a>—<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=D._Montes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="D. Montes (page does not exist)">D. Montes</a>, UCM</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.space.com/scienceastronomy/060703_mystery_monday.html" rel="nofollow">Wild Weather: Iron Rain on Failed Stars</a>—scientists are investigating astonishing weather patterns on brown dwarfs, Space.com, 2006</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/vision/universe/starsgalaxies/brown_dwarf_detectives.html" rel="nofollow">NASA Brown dwarf detectives</a>—Detailed information in a simplified sense</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.darkstar1.co.uk/ds3.htm" rel="nofollow">Brown Dwarfs</a>—Website with general information about brown dwarfs (has many detailed and colorful artist's impressions)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stars">Stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brown_dwarf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
